line=9  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16:ACKNOWLEDGMENTS

doc_id=94.tetml page=2 block_id=2-4 bbox=67.4,504.1,541.8,651.0 10 16
line=10  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The investigations reported here are the result of three lucky events. The first occurred in 1986.  I
line=11  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:had recently done the work reported in Pesetsky (1987), and received in the mail a copy of Kiss (1986).
line=12  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Since I had argued at length that D-linked wh-phrases do not display Superiority effects. I was astonished
line=13  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:by a paradigm reported by Kiss, which appears here as example (98). These facts remained stubbornly in
line=14  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:my mind for the next decade as an unsolved puzzle. Kiss did not publish this paper in the form that I
line=15  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:received — and, in fact, did not even recall discovering the crucial facts when she heard this work presented
line=16  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:as a talk in 1998. But the facts are hers nonetheless.

doc_id=94.tetml page=2 block_id=2-5 bbox=67.4,301.3,544.6,470.6 17 24
line=17  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       The second lucky event occurred in Spring 1994. I had just taught my own Pesetsky (1987) to a
line=18  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :graduate syntax class, but had not assigned a problem set on it. The next topic on the syllabus was
line=19  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Antecedent Contained Deletion, which I was teaching for the first time. I needed a problem set that covered
line=20  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :both topics, and the idea popped into my head that perhaps Pesetsky (1987) might make a prediction about
line=21  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:Antecedent Contained Deletion in wh phrases that should have moved overtly but didn't – a prediction which
line=22  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :might serve as the topic of a single, unified problem set on the two topics. The result was the observation in
line=23  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :(60) and (61). A conversation with Chris Kennedy (who had been thinking about this issue independently)
line=24  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :reinforced my interest in this contrast.

doc_id=94.tetml page=2 block_id=2-6 bbox=67.4,145.1,544.6,267.8 25 30
line=25  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       Finally, in 1996 (a decade after I filed Kiss's facts away in my memory), I was lucky enough to hear
line=26  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:a presentation of Beck (1996) and Beck and Kim (1996) by Sigrid Beck at an informal meeting of MIT's
line=27  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:student-organized "LF Reading Group". Her observations immediately reminded me of Kiss's
line=28  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:observations. The present paper grew out of my attempt to understand the connection between these two sets
line=29  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of observations – an effort which led me right back to the data from my problem set on Antecedent
line=30  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Contained Deletion.

doc_id=94.tetml page=2 block_id=2-7 bbox=67.4,78.1,534.4,111.6 31 32
line=31  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       My proposals have undergone several transformations, with the result that some whose comments
line=32  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:are acknowledged in this work may not recognize it as the descendent of the one that they heard.  I am

line=68  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:to acknowledge loose ends, unexplored alternatives, and interesting corollaries without interrupting the main
line=69  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:lines of an already intricate investigation. If they distract, ignore.

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-3 bbox=67.4,694.7,155.1,707.7 73 73
line=73  tag=O fonts=F0-13.02:1. Introduction

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-4 bbox=67.4,467.8,539.0,657.5 74 82
line=74  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :       This study is an attempt to discover the types of movement and movement-like relations that link
line=75  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :positions in syntactic structure. I will argue that we can identify at least two kinds of movement-like
line=76  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :relations: phrasal movement of the traditional kind, and the relation that Chomsky (1995, chapter 4) has
line=77  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:called "feature movement.1 My results differ sharply from Chomsky's, however, in supporting an
line=78  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :additional distinction between overt and covert phrasal movement. If these conclusions are correct, then
line=79  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :"feature movement" is not the proper reanalysis of covert phrasal movement, as Chomsky suggested, but is
line=80  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :a phenomenon in its own right — a type of syntactic relation distinct from previously studied instances of
line=81  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :movement. All in all, then, we will see evidence for three kinds of movement relations: overt phrasal
line=82  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :movement, covert phrasal movement, and feature movement.

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-5 bbox=100.9,422.2,519.5,433.4 83 83
line=83  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:The focus of our study will be wh-questions. In particular, our classification of movement-like

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-6 bbox=67.4,285.5,530.8,409.2 84 89
line=84  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:relations will allow us to understand the story behind wh-questions in which an otherwise inviolable
line=85  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:property of movement — "Attract Closest" — appears to be violated. We will see that Attract Closest is
line=86  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:actually not violated at all in the cases in question. The key to this conclusion will be a demonstration that
line=87  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:more movement takes place in these configurations than we might have suspected, so that the "closest"
line=88  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:element is really being attracted after all. Crucially, we will be able to reach this conclusion only once we
line=89  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:accept the expanded repertoire of movement-like relations presented in this study.

doc_id=94.tetml page=5 block_id=5-7 bbox=67.4,81.8,543.8,251.1 90 97
line=90  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The study concludes with a corollary investigation of a semantic restriction on wh-questions
line=91  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:investigated by Beck (1996). This restriction appears in English in exactly those constructions that occupy
line=92  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:us in the first sections of this study, but has a wider distribution in German — and an even wider
line=93  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:distribution in Japanese and Korean. I will try to show that the distribution of Beck's effect in these three
line=94  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:language-types follows straightforwardly from our hypothesis about the movement-like relations available to
line=95  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:languages, in tandem with a typology of specifier requirements that also figures prominently in the first part
line=96  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of the study. In short, I will argue that our general view of movement helps us understand how the syntax
line=97  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of wh-questions differs across languages.

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-2 bbox=316.7,749.6,329.7,760.7 98 98
line=98  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-6-

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-3 bbox=100.9,693.8,369.8,704.9 99 99
line=99  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:I begin with an introduction to the central issues of the study.

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-4 bbox=84.2,648.2,286.6,659.4 100 100
line=100  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16:1.1.Phrasal movement: overt and covert

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-5 bbox=67.4,443.6,543.8,613.8 101 108
line=101  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :       The term "movement" describes a situation in which a syntactic unit — for example, a phrase —
line=102  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :appears to occupy more than one point in syntactic structure. Movement has been most easily detected by
line=103  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :researchers when a word or phrase is pronounced in a position where we do not expect it to be pronounced
line=104  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :(given an independently well-motivated syntax for argument-taking and modification).  Often, when a word
line=105  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51,F2-11.16:or phrase is pronounced in an "unexpected" position, it is pronounced only in the unexpected position.2 It
line=106  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :is not additionally pronounced in its expected ("trace") position. As was observed in the early 1970s, the
line=107  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                 :pronunciation position in these cases typically c-commands the trace position. This constellation of syntactic
line=108  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51,F2-11.16:and phonological properties constitutes what we can call overt phrasal movement.3

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-6 bbox=67.4,350.6,545.6,408.3 109 111
line=109  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:       Overt phrasal movement is movement in that it obeys a characteristic set of command restrictions, as
line=110  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :well as locality conditions governing the distance between the "expected" position and the position created by
line=111  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:movement. It is phrasal in that the moved unit is a word or group of words. (Reconstruction effects reveal

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-7 bbox=67.4,234.3,541.9,337.6 112 116
line=112  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:the presence of the moved phrase in both trace position and targeted position.) It is overt in that it affects the
line=113  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :phonology; the sentence sounds different with the movement operation than it would have sounded without
line=114  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :it.  Many of the best-studied instances of movement belong to the category of overt phrasal movement. Of
line=115  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :special interest to us will be overt wh-phrase movement of the sort familiar from many languages, including
line=116  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :English:

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-8 bbox=67.4,167.4,253.5,178.6 117 117
line=117  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:(1) Which book did Mary give __ to Sue?

doc_id=94.tetml page=6 block_id=6-9 bbox=67.4,78.1,545.6,133.9 118 120
line=118  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       As linguists, we are lucky that overt phrasal movement exists, or else movement itself might not have
line=119  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:been discovered. Nonetheless, from a broader perspective, the pronunciation pattern of overt phrasal
line=120  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:movement seems rather arbitrary. Why should pronunciation target the moved position and not the trace

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-2 bbox=316.7,749.6,329.7,760.7 121 121
line=121  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-7-

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-3 bbox=67.4,625.9,545.6,727.2 122 126
line=122  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :position? Why not the other way around, for example? In fact, a large body of research has provided
line=123  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :evidence that other pronunciation patterns do exist. The proposals of Chomsky (1976), May (1977; 1984)
line=124  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :and Huang (1981; 1982) provided central arguments for the existence of alternative pronunciation patterns
line=125  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :for movement. These researchers argued that sometimes a moved element is pronounced in a trace position,
line=126  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:rather than in its final (highest) position. This type of movement is traditionally called covert. We will call it

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-4 bbox=67.4,485.4,537.2,612.9 127 132
line=127  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:covert phrasal movement. Covert phrasal movement can be shown to be movement insofar as it creates a
line=128  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :link between positions which obeys command restrictions and islands in a manner familiar from overt
line=129  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:phrasal movement. Covert phrasal movement is covert in that it does not affect the segmental phonology.
line=130  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :The moved words affected by covert phrasal movement are pronounced just as if no movement had taken
line=131  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:place. Covert phrasal movement is phrasal in that entire words and word groups are copied from the trace
line=132  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :position to the new position.

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-5 bbox=67.4,396.2,535.4,452.0 133 135
line=133  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       A particularly good argument for covert phrasal movement comes from "antecedent-contained"
line=134  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:anaphora of the sort familiar from research on Antecedent-Contained Deletion (ACD) constructions like (2)
line=135  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:(Bouton 1970; Sag 1976; May 1985; Larson and May 1990):

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-6 bbox=67.4,299.4,324.1,339.4 136 136
line=136  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16,F1-9.3,F3-11.16:(2)    ACDMary [_VP invited [_DP everyone that I did [_VP ∆ ]]]

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-7 bbox=67.4,226.9,536.0,261.3 137 138
line=137  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:The argument runs as follows. The example in (2) most naturally has the interpretation in (3) (where "t" is
line=138  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :the trace of relativization within DP):

doc_id=94.tetml page=7 block_id=7-8 bbox=67.4,156.2,334.3,192.5 139 140
line=139  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(3)     Interpretation of ellipsis in (2)
line=140  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-9.3  :       Mary [_VP invited [_DP everyone that I [_VP  invited t ]]

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-2 bbox=316.7,749.6,329.7,760.7 141 141
line=141  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-8-

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-3 bbox=67.4,625.9,539.1,704.9 142 145
line=142  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :It is a fact about VP-ellipsis (Hankamer and Sag 1976) that an elided VP requires a linguistic expression as
line=143  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :its antecedent. In contrast to a pronoun, which may take its reference from a contextually salient, but
line=144  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :unmentioned individual , an elided VP can only take a pronounced VP as its antecedent. Thus, while I may
line=145  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:say "Thank goodness he's left" as a response to the departure of an salient but unmentioned individual, I

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-4 bbox=67.4,597.0,492.4,610.1 146 146
line=146  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-9.3,F2-11.16,F3-11.16:cannot say "Thank goodness I didn't [_VP ∆ ]]" as a response to someone's tripping over a wire.

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-5 bbox=67.4,570.1,524.2,581.2 147 147
line=147  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:Consequently, the fact that the elided VP in (3) is understood as the expression invited t tells us that the

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-6 bbox=67.4,499.4,544.7,557.1 148 150
line=148  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:linguistic context must contain an antecedent of the form "invited t". If the analysis of (2) does not involve
line=149  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :covert movement, then we cannot understand the availability of interpretation (3). Without covert movement,
line=150  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:(2) contains no VP of the form invited t — only a VP of the form invited everyone that I did. If, on the

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-7 bbox=67.4,475.2,533.5,486.4 151 151
line=151  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:other hand, the phrase everyone that I did undergoes covert phrasal movement to a VP-external position,

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-8 bbox=67.4,451.0,537.2,462.2 152 152
line=152  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:then it will leave behind a VP of the form invited t, supplying the appropriate antecedent for the elided VP:

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-9 bbox=67.4,404.5,316.5,415.7 153 153
line=153  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(4)    Example (2) after covert phrasal movement

doc_id=94.tetml page=8 block_id=8-10 bbox=100.9,378.5,358.5,391.5 154 154
line=154  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-9.3,F2-11.16:[_DP everyone that I [_VP invited t ]] [Mary [_VP invited t ]]

doc_id=94.tetml page=9 block_id=9-5 bbox=67.4,489.2,544.6,656.6 167 174
line=167  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The story is more complex than this, of course, if traces are simply the originals of which moved
line=168  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:phrases are copies (Chomsky 1995, chapter 3). In the process of semantic interpretation, descriptive
line=169  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:material in this trace position must be deleted, so that the trace not only functions as a variable, but also helps
line=170  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:provide an appropriate antecedent VP for the ellipsis site. Fox (1995), building on a discussion by Fiengo
line=171  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:and May (1994), shows that this process (which, he argues, is motivated by ACD interpretation) interacts
line=172  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:with Principle C of the Binding Theory just as expected. Covert movement in normal circumstances is
line=173  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:insufficient to bleed Principle C (presumably because nothing motivates deletion of the trace-internal material
line=174  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:that generates the Principle C violation):

doc_id=94.tetml page=12 block_id=12-7 bbox=301.8,574.7,429.8,585.9 241 241
line=241  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:(Fiengo and May 1994, 242)

doc_id=94.tetml page=12 block_id=12-8 bbox=67.4,440.8,541.9,541.2 242 246
line=242  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       Let us now consider the mechanics of covert phrasal movement in more detail. Traditionally, the
line=243  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:pronunciation difference between overt and covert phrasal movement has been viewed as a consequence of
line=244  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:the timing of movement within a derivational model. In the model assumed in many studies — the so-called
line=245  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:"(inverted) Y-model" of Chomsky and Lasnik (1977) — the pronunciation of the chains of positions linked
line=246  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:by movement is governed by a simple principle of Phonological Spellout:

doc_id=94.tetml page=12 block_id=12-9 bbox=67.4,372.0,355.9,406.4 247 248
line=247  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(13)   Phonological Spellout in the "Y-model":
line=248  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       Pronounce only the highest position in a movement chain.

doc_id=94.tetml page=12 block_id=12-10 bbox=67.4,124.6,542.7,338.5 249 258
line=249  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :On this view, covert movement is simply movement that takes place after Spellout . Movement after
line=250  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Spellout thus creates a situation in which the highest position at LF is higher than the highest position at the
line=251  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :time of Spellout. Phonological processing and covert movement take place on separate derivational tracks
line=252  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :(the arms of the inverted "Y"). Though this view is common, few (if any) sustainable arguments seem to
line=253  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :support the Y-model's segregation of overt and covert movement within the syntax. Indeed, the segregation
line=254  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :of overt and covert movement into separate blocks of structure-building operations creates complications for
line=255  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :simple views of structure-building like that advanced by Chomsky (1995, chapter 3-4), in which the phrase
line=256  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:structure of an expression simply is its derivational history. Covert movement as analyzed within the Y-
line=257  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :model would be fairly unique in "altering the historical record" — by tinkering with the internal structure of
line=258  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :the derivation.

doc_id=94.tetml page=13 block_id=13-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=13 block_id=13-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 259 259
line=259  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-13-

doc_id=94.tetml page=13 block_id=13-3 bbox=67.4,470.6,545.6,727.2 260 271
line=260  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       In Pesetsky (1997; 1998), I suggested an alternative view, which places the burden of accounting for
line=261  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:the covert/overt distinction on the phonology (Similar ideas have also been developed by Bobaljik 1995,
line=262  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:who calls this view "Single Output Syntax", and by Groat and O'Neil 1996. A precursor was Brody 1995,
line=263  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:circulated in 1992.)  In this view, the syntax is simpler than in the Y-model; there is just one movement
line=264  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:component. The trade-off comes in the phonological component, where the simple principle of Phonological
line=265  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Spellout in (13) is replaced by phonological principles of chain pronunciation that regulate the pronunciation
line=266  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of moved elements in a more complex manner. These principles determine, for example, whether the head
line=267  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of a chain is the only position pronounced (overt phrasal movement) or whether a trace position will be
line=268  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:pronounced instead (covert phrasal movement). An indirect but telling argument for this point of view was
line=269  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:the observation that the dichotomy "overt vs. covert" inherent in the "Y-model" is too crude, since there are
line=270  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:other pronunciation patterns for chains. Sometimes, no position within a chain is pronounced, and
line=271  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:sometimes more than one chain position is pronounced (e.g. in resumptive pronoun constructions).

doc_id=94.tetml page=13 block_id=13-4 bbox=67.4,267.8,539.4,437.1 272 279
line=272  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:       In this study, very little will depend on the choice between the Y-model and Single-Output Syntax.7
line=273  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :Nonetheless, the overall picture will be significantly easier to discuss within the architecturally simpler
line=274  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :Single-Output model. I will therefore assume that there is only one cycle of syntactic movement, and will
line=275  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :describe pronunciation distinctions like the distinction between overt and covert phrasal movement as
line=276  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :essentially phonological. Against this backdrop, I will argue that (14) and (15) give the proper
line=277  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :characterization of the difference between English and Bulgarian pronunciation patterns in multiple
line=278  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :questions. Justification of the reference to the "first instance" and "secondary instances" of wh-movement
line=279  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :will be offered shortly.

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 280 280
line=280  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-14-

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-3 bbox=67.4,692.8,241.0,704.0 281 281
line=281  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(14)   Pronunciation rule (English):

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-4 bbox=100.9,598.9,543.7,679.8 282 285
line=282  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:a. The first instance of wh-phrase movement to C is overt in that wh is pronounced in its new
line=283  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :   position, and unpronounced in its trace positions..
line=284  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:b. Secondary instances of wh-phrase movement to C are covert, in that wh is pronounced in its trace
line=285  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :   position, and is unpronounced in its new position.

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-5 bbox=67.4,553.3,254.3,564.5 286 286
line=286  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(15)   Pronunciation rule (Bulgarian):

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-6 bbox=100.9,505.9,490.6,540.3 287 288
line=287  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:All wh-phrase movement to C is overt, in that wh is pronounced in its new position, and
line=288  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :unpronounced in its trace positions.

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-7 bbox=84.2,438.0,199.0,449.2 289 289
line=289  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16:1.2.Feature movement

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-8 bbox=67.4,189.7,543.8,403.6 290 299
line=290  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       In his first paper developing the "Minimalist Program", Chomsky (1995, chapter 3) suggested that
line=291  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :covert phrasal movement is the default style of movement. This idea was implemented by means of a
line=292  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:principle called Procrastinate, which required movement to be covert. This principle, in turn, was argued
line=293  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :to be overruled whenever movement is motivated by a "strong" feature. A strong feature, in this system,
line=294  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :was a feature that needed to be "checked off" by overt movement, in order to avoid violating the principle of
line=295  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Full Interpretation at PF. Though this proposal provoked much interesting research and discussion, it had a
line=296  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :certain arbitrariness about it, in that it was not obvious (at least at the conceptual level) why language should
line=297  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :incorporate a timing principle like Procrastinate. Why not the opposite timing principle (e.g. the Earliness
line=298  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Principle of Pesetsky 1989, as incorporated, for example, in Brody's 1995 "radically minimalist" model), or
line=299  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :no timing principle at all?

doc_id=94.tetml page=14 block_id=14-9 bbox=67.4,78.1,536.3,156.2 300 303
line=300  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       In later work, Chomsky (1995, chapter 4) offered an alternative view that promised to eliminate the
line=301  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:issue. He suggested that it had been a mistake to assume that covert movement is ever "phrasal".
line=302  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Chomsky's 1995 proposal starts with the idea that movement is a "repair strategy" by which an
line=303  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:uninterpretable feature F on a head K is deleted in response to movement to K of another instance of F

doc_id=94.tetml page=15 block_id=15-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=15 block_id=15-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 304 304
line=304  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-15-

doc_id=94.tetml page=15 block_id=15-3 bbox=67.4,535.7,540.1,727.2 305 313
line=305  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :(typically an interpretable instance of F.. Failure to repair a structure that contains an uninterpretable feature
line=306  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :renders the derivation non-convergent. Movement for any other purpose is banned. Thus, for example, an
line=307  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:uninterpretable wh feature on C might require movement of the corresponding wh feature from a wh-phrase
line=308  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :elsewhere in the structure, but there could be no "gratuitous" movement of this feature in other
line=309  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :circumstances. Likewise, an uninterpretable person-feature on T requires movement of a person-feature
line=310  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :from some DP internal to the structure containing T (with gratuitous instances of this movement strategy
line=311  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :prohibited). On this view, movement at its simplest should copy just the features necessary to ensure
line=312  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :convergence (Chomsky 1995, 262).  Copying of anything more than features is unexpected — especially
line=313  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :the phonological features and dependent constituents copied in overt phrasal movement.

doc_id=94.tetml page=15 block_id=15-4 bbox=67.4,310.6,541.8,502.2 314 322
line=314  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :       In this system, phrasal movement is, in a sense, the surprise. Phrasal movement is a situation in
line=315  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :which a grammatical feature that must be moved cannot be separated from the syntactic expression in which
line=316  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :it occurs. In the case of overt DP movement to subject position (SPEC of TP), for example, it is supposed
line=317  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :that the D feature cannot be copied apart from the remainder of the expression that it labels. Likewise, overt
line=318  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :wh-phrase movement is attributed to the inability of the phonological system to pronounce the wh-feature
line=319  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :and the remainder of its phrase in separate places. Chomsky suggests that when F moves, "F carries along
line=320  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :just enough material for convergence". Movement of more than just the relevant feature occurs only if
line=321  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :"generalized pied piping" is necessary in order for the derivation to converge at LF or PF. Chomsky further
line=322  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:speculates that only PF considerations force pied piping of this sort.8

doc_id=94.tetml page=15 block_id=15-5 bbox=67.4,85.5,541.8,277.1 323 331
line=323  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       Chomsky's 1995 proposal leaves us with two types of movement. Movement that involves copying
line=324  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :of a phrase is motivated by PF considerations, and must perforce be "overt" movement.  Movement that
line=325  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :copies only the grammatical features that motivate the movement must perforce be "covert" movement. On
line=326  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :this view, then, feature movement is the simply the proper analysis of covert movement — "covert" in the
line=327  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:sense that it has no effect on the phonology. The postulation of covert phrasal movement, from this
line=328  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :perspective, was simply an error. Notice that this view, if correct, offers a third slant on the overt/covert
line=329  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :contrast — distinct both from the "Y-model" tradition and from the "single-output" syntax literature. The
line=330  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :proposal that covert movement is feature movement removes any need for either syntactic timing or
line=331  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :phonological principles to distinguish the two movement types.9 Covert and overt movement may

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 332 332
line=332  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-16-

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-3 bbox=67.4,649.1,536.4,727.2 333 336
line=333  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:participate in a single syntactic cycle, with the phonological distinctions arising simply from the differences
line=334  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:between the material copied by the movement operation. Chomsky (1995, chapter 4) did not develop this
line=335  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:consequence of his new view of movement, but it follows rather straightforwardly, nonetheless. In more
line=336  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:recent work, Chomsky (1998) takes exactly this step.

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-4 bbox=67.4,468.7,545.6,615.6 337 343
line=337  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       Chomsky's (1995, chapter 4) proposals concerning movement are consonant with some of the
line=338  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :arguments for covert movement, but not with all of them. It is, of course, consonant with any test that cares
line=339  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :only about the relation between a source and a target — for example, the command condition discussed in
line=340  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :connection with (8). The new view runs afoul, however, of evidence for covert movement that identifies the
line=341  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :moving element as a word or phrase. As we saw, evidence from ACD has just this property. ACD
line=342  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:resolution requires movement of an entire phrase out of categories that contain it (a conclusion supported by
line=343  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :the Binding Theory evidence of Fiengo and May (1994) as explained by Fox 199510).

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-5 bbox=67.4,154.4,544.7,435.2 344 356
line=344  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       Consequently, it does not appear correct to simply reanalyze covert phrasal movement as feature
line=345  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :movement. Covert phrasal movement exists. I suspect that this is not a step backwards. Although
line=346  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Chomsky's 1995 proposal provides a rationale for his earlier assumption that covert movement is the default
line=347  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :type of movement, it is not clear that the earlier assumption was correct.  I know of no empirical evidence
line=348  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :bearing on the matter, nor is it obvious how the conceptual chips fall. Indeed, alongside the plausiblesounding
line=349  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :         idea that the most natural style of movement would copy just the features that are necessary in
line=350  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :order to ensure convergence, one might lay a quite different, equally plausible idea: that movement copies
line=351  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:the largest constituent that bears the relevant feature. This idea would make phrasal movement (rather than
line=352  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :feature movement) the default. Each of these ideas is natural. Let us view movement as a process by which
line=353  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :a head H in search of a feature F scans down the tree in order to identify a constituent that bears F and copies
line=354  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :it. According to Chomsky's 1999 idea, only the feature itself is copied; according to the alternative, the
line=355  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :identified constituent is copied. I am hard-pressed to find even an Occam's Razor argument that favors one
line=356  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :idea over the other.11

doc_id=94.tetml page=16 block_id=16-6 bbox=67.4,87.4,545.6,120.9 357 358
line=357  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       On the other hand, whatever one's views of the actual proposals, one can distill an important
line=358  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:question from the discussion. Granted that movement, both overt and covert, does sometimes copy phrases,

doc_id=94.tetml page=17 block_id=17-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=17 block_id=17-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 359 359
line=359  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-17-

doc_id=94.tetml page=17 block_id=17-3 bbox=67.4,625.0,531.6,727.2 360 364
line=360  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :are there movement operations that simply establish a relationship between different expressions bearing a
line=361  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:particular feature? In this study, I will suggest that the answer is yes. If so, then Chomsky's (1995,
line=362  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :chapter 4) discussion of feature movement uncovered something real — but misidentified the discovery.
line=363  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :The phenomenon called "feature movement" by Chomsky is not an alternative analysis of covert phrasal
line=364  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :movement, as he thought, but is a distinct syntactic operation in its own right.12

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 422 422
line=422  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-20-

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-3 bbox=67.4,577.5,544.6,726.3 423 429
line=423  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       In the case of the there-construction, the feature movement hypothesis revises an earlier set of
line=424  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :proposals, also by Chomsky, in which the movement properties of the associate-T relation were attributed to
line=425  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :covert phrasal movement. Though ACD did not form one of Chomsky's arguments for this revision, it
line=426  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :provides a strong argument in its favor. If we accept this conclusion, however, we need an explanation for
line=427  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :the contrast between the "associate in situ" in the there-construction and wh-in-situ in multiple questions. In
line=428  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :each case, we found evidence of a relation between a phrase pronounced in situ and a higher position. But
line=429  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:the two constructions differ in their behavior under a test for specifically phrasal covert movement. While

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-4 bbox=67.4,553.3,544.7,564.5 430 430
line=430  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:covert movement of the associate in the there-construction looks like feature movement, covert movement of

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-5 bbox=67.4,505.9,527.0,540.3 431 432
line=431  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:wh-in-situ looks like phrasal movement. It is this pattern of evidence that suggests the existence of more
line=432  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:than one type of movement-like relationship between syntactic positions.

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-6 bbox=67.4,236.2,543.8,472.4 433 443
line=433  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       I will take these conclusions as my point of departure, devoting considerable attention to distinctions
line=434  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:between phrasal movement and "feature movement" in the sense of Chomsky (1995, chapter 4). Later in
line=435  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:this study, however, we will also examine some possible variants this picture — though I will leave the
line=436  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:choice among alternatives open. In particular, I will entertain the possibility that "feature movement" is
line=437  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:actually a subcomponent of phrasal movement. I will attribute the similarities between the two operations to
line=438  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:that fact, rather than to the existence of two substantially different varieties of movement.  Note, for
line=439  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:example, that there is no clear evidence that features of the associate in the there-construction actually
line=440  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:"move" to a position near T. Instead, the evidence points to some sort of communication between the
line=441  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:features of the associate and the features of T. For now, however, I will stick to more familiar concepts like
line=442  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:"movement", and will present my arguments as evidence for the coexistence of phrasal and feature
line=443  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:movement in grammar.

doc_id=94.tetml page=20 block_id=20-7 bbox=67.4,80.0,539.1,202.7 444 449
line=444  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       We are now in a position to sketch the goals of the study in some detail. If our discussion so far is
line=445  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:on the right track, we might expect to find "minimal triplets" in which a particular head is seen to establish a
line=446  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:relationship with a remote feature in each of the three ways we have seen—the choice depending on other
line=447  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:properties of the grammar. That is, we might expect to find movement to a particular head showing up
line=448  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:sometimes as overt phrasal movement, sometimes as covert phrasal movement, and sometimes as feature
line=449  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:movement. The existence of such a "minimal triplet" is not logically entailed by our hypotheses.

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 450 450
line=450  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-21-

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-3 bbox=67.4,671.5,541.9,727.2 451 453
line=451  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Nonetheless, if we can identify such a triplet, we will significantly improve our ability to investigate the
line=452  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:coexistence of phrasal movement with feature movement in the grammar, since we will have the opportunity
line=453  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:to examine the differences among these syntactic operations under well-controlled conditions.

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-4 bbox=67.4,603.6,545.6,638.0 454 455
line=454  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The remainder of this study presents and investigates a triplet of exactly this sort. In particular, I will
line=455  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:argue that interrogative wh-constructions involve relations of all three types. We have already seen examples

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-5 bbox=67.4,579.4,534.4,590.5 456 456
line=456  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of overt and covert phrasal wh-movement to C in English. I will suggest that under certain circumstances,

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-6 bbox=67.4,532.0,537.2,566.4 457 458
line=457  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:we can detect another kind of operation that relates wh-phrases to an interrogative C: feature movement (or
line=458  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:something very much like it).

doc_id=94.tetml page=21 block_id=21-7 bbox=67.4,167.4,542.7,498.5 459 473
line=459  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :        I introduced Bulgarian into our discussion of English multiple questions with malice aforethought,
line=460  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :since Bulgarian provides some of the foundations of our argument. After presenting some evidence in favor
line=461  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :of covert phrasal movement for wh-in-situ, I noted (in the footsteps of Lasnik and Saito 1984) that
line=462  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Bulgarian, on this view, simply shows overt phrasal movement where English shows its covert counterpart.
line=463  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :The difference between the two languages was summarized in (14)-(15). In the next section, I present
line=464  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :certain additional facts about Bulgarian multiple questions, for which partial explanations will be offered
line=465  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :(borrowed from Richards 1997). In the following sections, I will show that a seemingly distinct set of
line=466  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :puzzles concerning English multiple questions actually represents exactly the same phenomena we have just
line=467  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :observed in Bulgarian. If this is true, we can claim that the pronunciation distinctions in (14) and (15)
line=468  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:constitute the only relevant difference between multiple questions in the two languages. We only reach this
line=469  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :conclusion, however, if we assume that multiple questions in English (and Bulgarian) in certain cases
line=470  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:involves pure feature movement of wh — even though in other cases it involves overt and covert phrasal
line=471  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :movement triggered by the same feature. In the final sections of the study, we consider some syntactic and
line=472  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :semantic consequences of this hypothesis, which helps establish a cross-linguistic typology of whmovement
line=473  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :          .

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 474 474
line=474  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-22-

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-3 bbox=84.2,715.2,197.2,726.3 475 475
line=475  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16:1.3.Superiority effects

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-4 bbox=67.4,646.3,536.3,680.8 476 477
line=476  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The English puzzles involve the observation known as the "Superiority Effect".  The Superiority
line=477  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Effect arises in multiple questions when more than one wh-phrase is relevant to the answering patterns of a

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-5 bbox=67.4,622.2,541.8,633.3 478 478
line=478  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:given question. In such cases the syntax needs to decide on a pattern of wh-movement within that question.

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-6 bbox=67.4,574.7,514.4,609.1 479 480
line=479  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:The Superiority Effect is a restriction on this decision.  In English, where only one wh-phrase moves
line=480  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:overtly, the Superiority Effect is the contrast observed in (26)-(27):

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-7 bbox=67.4,483.6,222.1,540.3 481 483
line=481  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(26)   Superiority Effect
line=482  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :      a. Who __ bought what?
line=483  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :      b. *What did who buy __ ?

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-8 bbox=67.4,416.6,305.6,450.1 484 485
line=484  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:(27)   a. Who did you persuade __ to read what?
line=485  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:      b. ??What did you persuade whom to read __?

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-9 bbox=67.4,347.8,545.4,382.2 486 487
line=486  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:If we assume that the first application of wh-movement in these examples is the overt phrasal instance of whmovement
line=487  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:          , Kuno and Robinson's (1972) description of the phenomenon describes it accurately:

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-10 bbox=67.4,302.2,355.7,313.4 488 488
line=488  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:(28)   A wh word cannot be preposed crossing over another wh.

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-11 bbox=67.4,234.3,534.4,267.8 489 490
line=489  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Chomsky (1973) suggested that (28) is a special case of a more general phenomenon, which he termed the
line=490  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:"Superiority Condition:

doc_id=94.tetml page=22 block_id=22-12 bbox=67.4,120.0,477.7,199.9 491 494
line=491  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(29)   Superiority Condition (Chomsky 1973)
line=492  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       No rule can involve X, Y in the structure
line=493  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F3-9.3  :       ...X... [ _α ... Z... —WYV ...]
line=494  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       where the rule applies ambiguously to Z and Y and Z is superior to [m-commands] Y.

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 495 495
line=495  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-23-

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-3 bbox=67.4,671.5,545.6,727.2 496 498
line=496  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:The "Attract Closest" condition of Chomsky (1995, chapter 4) is, in essence, a restatement of the Superiority
line=497  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Condition for movement, where movement is viewed as triggered by particular features of a "target" head K.
line=498  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:I will abbreviate "Attract Closest" as AC:

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-4 bbox=67.4,625.9,407.7,637.0 499 499
line=499  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(30)   Attract Closest ("AC"; Chomsky 1995, chapter 4, pp. 280, 296)

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-5 bbox=100.9,598.9,538.6,610.1 500 500
line=500  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F3-11.16:α can raise to target K only if there is no legitimate operation Move β targeting K, where β is closer

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-6 bbox=100.9,571.0,123.2,582.2 501 501
line=501  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:to K.

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-7 bbox=67.4,457.6,537.2,537.5 502 505
line=502  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:When one presents the examples in (26)-(27) to speakers in a manner that is fair to the data (maintaining
line=503  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:focal stress on wh-in-situ in both cases, and presenting the examples without special context), the contrasts
line=504  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:are clear and stable. Nonetheless, there are minimal changes that one can make in these examples that
line=505  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:produce apparent exceptions to the Superiority Effect. These facts will be crucial to our investigation.

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-8 bbox=67.4,412.0,542.8,423.1 506 506
line=506  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F4-11.16:1.3.1 D-linking. One minimal change of this sort involves what Pesetsky (1987) called "D-linking". When

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-9 bbox=67.4,296.7,529.7,399.0 507 511
line=507  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :a wh-question asks for answers in which the individuals that replace the wh-phrases are drawn from a set
line=508  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :that is presumed to be salient both to speaker and hearer, the multiple question can appear to violate AC.
line=509  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :This possibility typically arises when the answers to the question are supposed to be drawn from a set of
line=510  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :individuals previously introduced into the discourse, or when the set forms part of the "common ground"
line=511  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:shared by speaker and hearer. Wh-phrases with which favor this type of interpretation:

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-10 bbox=67.4,204.6,319.3,261.3 512 514
line=512  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(31)   Superiority effect disappears with D-linking.
line=513  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :      a. Which person __ bought which book?
line=514  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :      b. Which book did which person buy __?

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-11 bbox=67.4,137.6,344.7,171.1 515 516
line=515  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:(32)   a. Which person did John talk to __ about which topic?
line=516  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       b. Which topic did John talk to which person about __?

doc_id=94.tetml page=23 block_id=23-12 bbox=67.4,68.8,532.4,103.2 517 518
line=517  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:The semantics of D-linked wh phrases closely tracks the semantics of the definite article the. Context sets
line=518  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :previously mentioned in the discourse qualify a phrase as D-linked, but so do sets that are merely salient

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 519 519
line=519  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-24-

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-3 bbox=67.4,715.2,544.7,726.3 520 520
line=520  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:(e.g. which book, in a context where speaker and hearer both know that reference is being made to a reading

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-4 bbox=67.4,691.0,537.9,702.1 521 521
line=521  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:list for a course), and sets whose salience is culturally determined (e.g. what day of the week, which sign

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-5 bbox=67.4,666.8,506.5,678.0 522 522
line=522  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:of the zodiac). A reliable rule of thumb is the following: if a wh-word in a multiple question can be

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-6 bbox=67.4,528.2,545.6,653.8 523 528
line=523  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:felicitously paraphrased with an expression of the form which of the X, it can cause the Superiority Effect to
line=524  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :disappear. The reason for this link between semantics and syntax is obscure, and will remain obscure even at
line=525  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :the end of this study. On the other hand, the syntax of the problem is also obscure: what is the structure of
line=526  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :D-linked questions16 in which the Superiority Effect appears to be obviated? Are they exceptions to Attract
line=527  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:Closest, or is something else going on? By the end of this study, I hope to have answered these questions
line=528  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :quite fully.

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-7 bbox=70.2,482.7,519.5,493.8 529 529
line=529  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51,F4-11.16:1.3.2 More than two wh-phrases. This factor is less studied,17 but equally striking. The Superiority

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-8 bbox=67.4,458.5,535.3,469.6 530 530
line=530  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Effect is limited to questions with two wh-phrases. It disappears in multiple questions with more than two

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-9 bbox=67.4,434.3,128.8,445.5 531 531
line=531  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:wh-phrases.18

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-10 bbox=67.4,342.2,424.6,399.0 532 534
line=532  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(33)   Superiority effect disappears with more than 2 wh-phrases.
line=533  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       a. *What did who give __ to Mary?  [Sensation of Superiority effect]
line=534  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       b. What did who give __ to whom?  [No sensation of Superiority effect]

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-11 bbox=67.4,228.8,534.4,308.8 535 538
line=535  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:We will discuss this effect in greater detail later. For now, we simply wish to note the important syntactic
line=536  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:question that this contrast raises. They are the same as the ones posed in the previous section: What is the
line=537  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:structure of questions with more than two wh-phrases, in which the Superiority Effect appears to be
line=538  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:obviated? Are they exceptions to Attract Closest, or is something else going on?

doc_id=94.tetml page=24 block_id=24-12 bbox=67.4,137.6,531.7,194.4 539 541
line=539  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F4-11.16:1.3.3 German. Another minimal change one can make in a multiple question that obviates the Superiority
line=540  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Effect is translation into German. In at least the simplest cases, German appears to lack the Superiority
line=541  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :contrast entirely:

doc_id=94.tetml page=25 block_id=25-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=25 block_id=25-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 542 542
line=542  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-25-

line=543  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(34)   Superiority effect disappears in German.
line=544  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       a. Ich weiß nicht, wer was gesehen hat. 'I don't know who has seen what.'
line=545  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       b. Ich weiß nicht, was wer gesehen hat. 'I don't know what who has seen.'

doc_id=94.tetml page=25 block_id=25-4 bbox=67.4,580.3,539.0,636.1 546 548
line=546  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:The correct interpretation of this fact is a matter of some current controversy, which we will turn to towards
line=547  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:the end of this study. For now, it is sufficient to observe the problem, and to ask the same questions we
line=548  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:have asked about the previous two sets of apparent counterexamples.

doc_id=94.tetml page=25 block_id=25-5 bbox=67.4,491.0,538.1,546.8 549 551
line=549  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       These are the problems that will occupy us for the remainder of this study.  In the next section, we
line=550  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:present a fuller picture of Bulgarian multiple questions, as necessary background to our discussion of
line=551  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:apparent exceptions to the Superiority Effect.

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-3 bbox=67.4,714.2,284.9,727.2 553 553
line=553  tag=O fonts=F0-13.02:2. Two Observations about Bulgarian

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-4 bbox=84.2,664.9,370.4,676.1 554 554
line=554  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16:2.1. A Complementizer That Requires Multiple Specifiers

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-5 bbox=67.4,528.2,542.9,629.6 555 559
line=555  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       I have already noted that Bulgarian (unlike English) shows multiple instances of overt phrasal whmovement
line=556  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:           to the left periphery of CP. I have not said anything so far about the obligatoriness or optionality
line=557  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:of multiple overt movement. The obligatoriness of multiple overt movement partly depends on D-linking, in
line=558  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:ways to which we return later. For now, let us consider only multiple questions without a D-linked
line=559  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:interpretation. In these cases, the facts are somewhat subtle.

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-6 bbox=100.9,482.7,528.7,493.8 560 560
line=560  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Consider a question containing three wh-phrases. According to consultants' judgments, the most

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-7 bbox=67.4,458.5,539.9,469.6 561 561
line=561  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:natural multiple question using three wh-phrases shows all three phrases overtly fronted, as in (35a) below.

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-8 bbox=67.4,434.3,510.2,445.5 562 562
line=562  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Nonetheless, a construction in which only two of the wh-phrases are fronted is fairly acceptable. By

doc_id=94.tetml page=26 block_id=26-9 bbox=67.4,319.9,541.9,421.3 563 567
line=563  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :contrast, a multiple question in which only one wh-phrase is overtly fronted is strongly unacceptable (in the
line=564  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :non-D-linked usage being considered). There is thus a three-way contrast. The diacritics ("**" vs. "??" vs.
line=565  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :"ok") are those supplied by one of my consultants, Roumyana Izvorski, but the general description of the
line=566  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :judgments is one that contrasts perfection with unnaturalness with total unacceptability: The break between
line=567  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:(35b-c) and (35d) is claimed to be sharper than that between (35a) and (35b-c).19

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 568 568
line=568  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-27-

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-3 bbox=67.4,715.2,312.5,726.3 569 569
line=569  tag=M+LN lang_name=Bulgarian lang_code=bul span_id=s1 fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:(35)   Bulgarian: all vs. 2 vs. 1 wh-phrase moves.

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-4 bbox=100.9,645.4,369.1,702.1 570 572
line=570  tag=L+CN lang_name=Bulgarian lang_code=bul span_id=s1 fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:a. Koj kakvo na kogo dade?       [allwh-phrases move]
line=571  tag=G    lang_name=Bulgarian lang_code=bul span_id=s1 fonts=F1-11.16         :  who what  to whom gave
line=572  tag=T    lang_name=Bulgarian lang_code=bul span_id=s1 fonts=F1-11.16         :  'Who gave what to whom?'

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-5 bbox=100.9,578.5,362.5,611.9 573 574
line=573  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:b. ??Koj na kogo dade kakvo?      [2 wh-phrases move]
line=574  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:c. ??Koj kakvo dade na kogo?      [2 wh-phrases move]

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-6 bbox=100.9,509.6,386.7,544.0 575 576
line=575  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:d. **Koj dade kakvo na kogo?      [only 1 wh-phrase moves]
line=576  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :                    (R. Izvorski, I. Derzhanski p.c.)

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-7 bbox=67.4,343.2,542.9,475.2 577 582
line=577  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                         :       Since there seems to be no reason to assume that the Bulgarian wh-phrases in a multiple question are
line=578  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                         :related to distinct heads (Rudin 1988), we may safely assume that these phrases all bear the specifier relation
line=579  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16                         :to the same interrogative head, in the manner posited by Koizumi (1995) and Ura (1996). Let us call this
line=580  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16,F2-11.16,F4-9.3:interrogative head the "multi-specifier complementizer", which I will abbreviate as C_m-spec — to indicate
line=581  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-9.3                  :that its "specifier potential" requires it to take more than one specifier. The ability of C_m-spec to attract more
line=582  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-9.3                  :than one wh-phrase indicates that the feature of C_m-spec responsible for wh-movement (uninterpretable

doc_id=94.tetml page=27 block_id=27-8 bbox=67.4,273.4,540.0,330.1 583 585
line=583  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :though it may be) does not need to delete and does not exhaust its ability to motivate wh-movement until its
line=584  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :maximal projection has been fully built. We are thus free to propose that (36) is true, and that (36) and (37)
line=585  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:are jointly responsible for the observed three-way split in judgments on Bulgarian multiple questions:20

line=670  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :        This is, in fact, the hypothesis of Richards (1997), who conducted an extensive study of multiple
line=671  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :attraction constructions in Bulgarian and other languages. As Richards pointed out, second instances of
line=672  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:movement to a given a tractor should "tuck in" if, in addition to the "attractor-oriented" condition Attract

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-10 bbox=67.4,291.1,538.9,348.8 673 675
line=673  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:Closest, movement also obeys a "mover-oriented" Shortest Move condition. The first wh-phrase to be
line=674  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :attracted by C in (44a) is, because of AC, the highest C in the structure. (Throughout this study, we shall
line=675  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:label the wh-phrase of a question which occupies the highest structural position before wh-movement wh1.

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-11 bbox=67.4,265.1,539.0,276.2 676 676

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-12 bbox=67.4,239.0,544.5,250.2 677 677
line=677  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:creates the first specifier of C.  The second wh to be attracted in constructions like (43)-(44) is the other whphrase

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-13 bbox=96.5,214.8,517.6,226.0 678 678
line=678  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:: wh2.  This wh-phrase could in principle create a specifier higher than wh1, or it could create a

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-14 bbox=67.4,188.8,538.0,199.9 679 679
line=679  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:specifier lower than wh1. Shortest Move favors creation of a specifier lower than wh1, i.e. "tucking in" as

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-15 bbox=67.4,140.4,535.4,174.8 680 681
line=680  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :in (45a). In this way, the interaction of AC and Shortest Move causes the command relations between two
line=681  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:wh-phrases after movement to mirror their order before movement.25

doc_id=94.tetml page=32 block_id=32-16 bbox=67.4,72.5,539.1,106.0 682 683
line=682  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       As Richards notes, a tucking-in derivation is "countercyclic" in that it does not extend the tree. This
line=683  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:would violate a "Strict Cycle" condition if each category were to count as a cycle — as suggested by

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 684 684
line=684  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-33-

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-3 bbox=67.4,491.0,543.7,727.2 685 695
line=685  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Chomsky (1995, chapter 1) under the rubric of the "Extension Condition", which requires each overt
line=686  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :operation to extend the tree at its root. Richards suggests that it is not the tucking-in hypothesis, but the
line=687  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Extension Condition that is incorrect.  The cases that actually motivated the Extension Condition and its
line=688  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :predecessors involved derivations in which the overt-movement requirements of a lower head are satisfied
line=689  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :later than the overt-movement requirements of a higher head. In later work, Chomsky (1995, chapter 4)
line=690  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :suggested an alternative approach that zeros in more tightly on the actual cases that need to be excluded. He
line=691  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :suggested that convergent derivations must satisfy the overt-movement requirements of a head H while still
line=692  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :building the portion of the tree headed by H. (In his terminology, failure to satisfy the "strong feature" of a
line=693  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:head H in a timely fashion "cancels the derivation".) This proposal, which Richards calls featural cyclicity,
line=694  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :blocks most traditional Strict Cycle violations, but allows tucking in as in (45a), precisely because tucking in
line=695  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :involves countercyclic movements to a single head.26

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-4 bbox=67.4,423.1,535.4,457.6 696 697
line=696  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       Now let us consider AC more closely — in particular, its effects in multiple questions with three or
line=697  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:more wh-phrases. All things being equal, one might expect AC to be obeyed in some fashion in each

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-5 bbox=67.4,373.9,533.5,410.1 698 699

line=699  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16       :       , one might expect their order after movement to mirror their order before movement — just as in a

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-6 bbox=67.4,349.7,540.9,360.8 700 700

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-7 bbox=67.4,325.5,523.1,336.6 701 701

doc_id=94.tetml page=33 block_id=33-8 bbox=67.4,301.3,545.8,312.5 702 702
line=702  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16,F4-11.16:after movement is not required to be the second highest wh before movement. The orders wh1 wh2 wh3 and

doc_id=94.tetml page=34 block_id=34-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 704 704

doc_id=94.tetml page=35 block_id=35-14 bbox=67.4,332.0,542.4,366.4 732 733
line=732  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :Richards (1997) argues that this view of the matter is exactly correct. He suggests further that the logic of
line=733  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:the "AC tax" is a special case of a much more general meta-constraint that he calls the Principle of Minimal

doc_id=94.tetml page=35 block_id=35-15 bbox=67.4,239.9,541.0,319.0 734 737
line=734  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:Compliance. I will not try to develop a precise characterization of this more general principle. (That task is
line=735  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :undertaken by Richards 1997). The phenomenon relevant to our concerns is the simple observation that
line=736  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :"attractor-oriented" constraints on movement apply only to the first movement operation that targets a given
line=737  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :attractor.

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-1 bbox=0,0,0,0 0 0

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-2 bbox=313.9,749.6,332.5,760.7 738 738
line=738  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:-36-

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-3 bbox=67.4,647.3,540.9,726.3 739 742
line=739  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F4-11.16:(50)   Principle of Minimal Compliance (PMC; Richards 1997)
line=740  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       For any dependency D that obeys constraint C, any elements that are relevant for determining
line=741  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       whether D obeys C can be ignored for the rest of the derivation for purposes of determining whether
line=742  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       any other dependency D’ obeys C.

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-4 bbox=100.9,511.5,545.5,612.9 743 747
line=743  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:An element X is relevant to determining whether a dependency D with head A and tail B obeys
line=744  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :constraint C iff
line=745  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       a. X is along the path of D (that is, X=A, X=B, or A c-commands X and X c-commands B).
line=746  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :          AND
line=747  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       b. X is a member of the class of elements to which C makes reference.

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-5 bbox=67.4,443.6,209.6,454.8 748 748
line=748  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F4-11.16:(51)   PMC for our purposes

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-6 bbox=100.9,416.6,518.6,427.8 749 749
line=749  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F3-11.16:Once an instance of movement to α has obeyed a constraint on the distance between source and

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-7 bbox=100.9,385.0,421.8,396.2 750 750
line=750  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F3-11.16:target, other instances of movement to α need not obey these constraints.

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-8 bbox=67.4,311.5,529.8,345.9 751 752
line=751  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       The PMC applies to a number of syntactic conditions. Certain island effects, for example, such as
line=752  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Ross's (1967) Complex NP Constraint, hold in their strongest form only of the first instance of whmovement

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-9 bbox=116.7,287.4,541.9,298.5 753 753
line=753  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:to a given C. Example (52a), for instance, displays the expected judgment for wh-movement out

doc_id=94.tetml page=36 block_id=36-10 bbox=67.4,195.3,533.5,274.3 754 757
line=754  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:of a clause contained inside an NP. Example (52b) contains exactly the same instance of wh movement as
line=755  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :(52a), except that here it is (by hypothesis) the second instance of movement to its target, rather than the
line=756  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :first. Strikingly, the effects on acceptability of the Complex NP Constraint violation fade27 to near the
line=757  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :vanishing point.

doc_id=94.tetml page=37 block_id=37-8 bbox=67.4,391.5,540.0,449.2 768 770
line=768  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:      These facts recall properties of English multiple questions. As is well-known, wh-in-situ in English
line=769  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:multiple questions do not display island effects — or at least display them very weakly (Hankamer 1974).
line=770  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:This fact has always been a puzzle, particularly if, as we have argued, wh-in-situ standardly undergo covert

doc_id=94.tetml page=37 block_id=37-9 bbox=67.4,367.4,514.0,378.5 771 771
line=771  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:wh-movement. Traditional accounts (e.g. Huang 1982) attribute the absence of island effects in these

doc_id=94.tetml page=37 block_id=37-10 bbox=67.4,296.7,542.8,354.3 772 774
line=772  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:environments to a special fact about covert wh-movement. As Richards notes, the phenomenon can instead
line=773  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:be seen as the English counterpart of the effect in (52). On this view, the absence of subjacency effects with
line=774  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:covert wh-movement in multiple questions is not due to the fact that the movement is covert, but to the fact

doc_id=94.tetml page=37 block_id=37-11 bbox=67.4,181.3,532.6,283.6 775 779
line=775  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51 :that it follows an earlier application of wh-movement in its clause.28
line=776  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       In this respect, Richard's Bulgarian discoveries are a perfect mirror of data from Italian discovered
line=777  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :by Longobardi (1991), as analyzed by Brody (1995). In Bulgarian we can see multiple instances of overt
line=778  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :movement, with the first instance paying the "subjacency tax" that allows later instances to by-pass the
line=779  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:subjacency effect. In Italian, we can observe multiple instances of covert movement with the same

doc_id=94.tetml page=37 block_id=37-12 bbox=67.4,157.2,529.7,168.3 780 780
line=780  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:properties. The Italian case does not involve wh-movement, but scope assignment to negative phrases. I

line=782  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:scope position (marked by non with post-verbal negation) can be separated by embedded clauses:

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-3 bbox=67.4,537.5,545.6,727.2 807 815
line=807  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :English, Bulgarian and Italian thus all display the same phenomenon: the first occurrence of movement to a
line=808  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :given position obeys islands, but not the second — whether the two movements differ in overtness
line=809  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F1-6.51:(English), are both overt (Bulgarian), or both covert (Italian). 29
line=810  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :       A cautionary note is in order.  Although AC is subject to the PMC, Shortest Move is another matter.
line=811  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :Once a given instance of movement to a given C has satisfied Shortest Move, it must not be the case that
line=812  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :subsequent instances of movement to that C may violate Shortest Move, or else we could not observe a
line=813  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :"tucking in" requirement. This follows if Shortest Move is not a constraint governing the distance between
line=814  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :an Attractor and Attractee, but rather a constraint that chooses the exact position near a given Attractor to
line=815  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16        :which copying takes place.

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-4 bbox=67.4,445.5,541.0,503.1 816 818
line=816  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16         :2.3    Summary of Bulgarian wh-movement
line=817  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       Let us collect and organize our observations concerning Bulgarian:
line=818  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:•      Multiple-Specifier requirement: The complementizer of a multiple question in Bulgarian appears

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-5 bbox=100.9,421.3,524.2,432.4 819 819
line=819  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:to strongly require at least two wh-phrases as specifiers. This is a separate requirement from the

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-6 bbox=100.9,397.1,400.4,408.3 820 820
line=820  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:general preference for moving all wh-phrases in a multiple question.

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-7 bbox=67.4,372.9,518.5,384.1 821 821
line=821  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:•      Superiority: The left-most wh-phrase in a multiple question is the wh-phrase that was highest

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-8 bbox=100.9,348.7,523.2,359.9 822 822
line=822  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:before movement — wh1 in our terminology. If this is the same fact as the Superiority Effect in

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-9 bbox=67.4,276.2,534.4,333.9 823 825
line=823  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       English, it suggests that the left-most wh-phrase is also the first to move. This entails that multiple
line=824  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       movement to a given head proceeds via "tucking in".
line=825  tag=O fonts=F0-11.16,F1-11.16:•      PMC: In Bulgarian, Attract Closest is only required to be satisfied by the first instance of phrasal

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-10 bbox=100.9,252.0,523.2,263.2 826 826
line=826  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:wh-movement to C. Consequently, the second wh-phrase to move is not necessarily the second

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-11 bbox=100.9,227.8,293.4,239.0 827 827
line=827  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:highest wh-phrase before movement (wh2).

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-12 bbox=67.4,157.2,542.3,191.6 828 829
line=828  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:Finally, we are now able to observe that the pronunciation principles in (14)-(15), repeated below, were
line=829  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:appropriately formulated — assuming, of course, that we were correct to identify the left-most wh-phrase in

doc_id=94.tetml page=39 block_id=39-13 bbox=67.4,133.0,342.8,144.1 830 830

line=970  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       Before turning to this question, however, I want to point out an interesting corollary result. The
line=971  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:comparison between (60) and (61) has revealed a link between the availability of ACD inside a wh-phrase

doc_id=94.tetml page=46 block_id=46-10 bbox=67.4,428.7,540.9,439.9 972 972

doc_id=94.tetml page=46 block_id=46-11 bbox=67.4,290.1,545.5,414.8 973 978
line=973  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :movement. Since it does not undergo covert phrasal movement, we can conclude a fortiori that it does not
line=974  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:undergo covert phrasal wh-movement, which is our main concern in this study. But it is worth noting some
line=975  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :implications of the stronger conclusion, because it bears directly on a current debate concerning the syntactic
line=976  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :configurations that resolve ACD. If we consider the various explanations for ACD resolution that have been
line=977  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :offered in the literature, and focus our attention on those explanations that posit covert phrasal movement,
line=978  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :we can distinguish two families of subproposals.

doc_id=94.tetml page=46 block_id=46-12 bbox=67.4,109.7,542.7,256.7 979 985
line=979  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :       One school of thought — represented, for example, by May (1985, 11-14) — has argued that ACD
line=980  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :resolution not only provides evidence of covert phrasal movement, but also reveals severe limitations on its
line=981  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16,F2-11.16:distribution. We can call this school of thought the limited movement family of theories. In particular,
line=982  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :May suggested that constraints on ACD teach us that covert phrasal movement is limited to special types of
line=983  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :constituents — constituents whose special syntactic or interpretive needs motivate covert phrasal movement.
line=984  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :The contrast between (67a) and (67b) was taken to show that quantified expressions, but not DPs headed by
line=985  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16         :proper names, undergo the covert phrasal movement that can resolve ACD:

line=1012  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:       These facts raise the question of whether, in Fiengo and May's words, "the construction can [still] be
line=1013  tag=O fonts=F1-11.16:taken as a diagnostic for quantification". They suggest that it can. For example, they take (70b) to argue in
